<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Common cold</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the low temperature, see <a href="/wiki/Cold" title="Cold">Cold</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Common viral infection of the upper respiratory tract</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Medical condition</div><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Cough" title="Cough">Cough</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sore_throat" title="Sore throat">sore throat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhinorrhea" title="Rhinorrhea">runny nose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fever" title="Fever">fever</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2015_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2015-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eccles2005_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eccles2005-4">[4]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Complication_(medicine)" title="Complication (medicine)">Complications</a></th><td class="infobox-data">Usually none, but occasionally <a href="/wiki/Otitis_media" title="Otitis media">otitis media</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sinusitis" title="Sinusitis">sinusitis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sepsis" title="Sepsis">sepsis</a> can occur<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ben2014_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ben2014-5">[5]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Usual onset</th><td class="infobox-data">~2 days from exposure<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CMAJ2014_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-CMAJ2014-6">[6]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Duration</th><td class="infobox-data">1–3 weeks<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2015_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2015-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heik2003_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heik2003-7">[7]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Causes</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">Viral</a> (usually <a href="/wiki/Rhinovirus" title="Rhinovirus">rhinovirus</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CE11_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-CE11-8">[8]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Medical_diagnosis" title="Medical diagnosis">Differential diagnosis</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Allergic_rhinitis" title="Allergic rhinitis">Allergic rhinitis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bronchitis" title="Bronchitis">bronchitis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bronchiolitis" title="Bronchiolitis">bronchiolitis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pertussis" title="Pertussis">pertussis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sinusitis" title="Sinusitis">sinusitis</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ben2014_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ben2014-5">[5]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Prevention</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Hand_washing" title="Hand washing">Hand washing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Surgical_mask" title="Surgical mask">face masks</a>, cough etiquette, <a href="/wiki/Social_distancing" title="Social distancing">avoiding sick people</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2015_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2015-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E209_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-E209-10">[10]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Treatment</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Symptomatic_treatment" title="Symptomatic treatment">Symptomatic therapy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2015_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2015-3">[3]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2016Zinc_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2016Zinc-11">[11]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Medication" title="Medication">Medication</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NSAIDs" title="NSAIDs">NSAIDs</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim2015_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kim2015-12">[12]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Frequency</th><td class="infobox-data">2–3 per year (adults); 6–8 per year (children)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFP07_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-AFP07-13">[13]</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>common cold</b>, also known simply as a <b>cold</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viral</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infectious_disease" title="Infectious disease">infectious disease</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_respiratory_tract" title="Upper respiratory tract">upper respiratory tract</a> that primarily affects the <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_epithelium" title="Respiratory epithelium">respiratory mucosa</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Human_nose" title="Human nose">nose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Throat" title="Throat">throat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paranasal_sinuses" title="Paranasal sinuses">sinuses</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Larynx" title="Larynx">larynx</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CMAJ2014_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-CMAJ2014-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CE11_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-CE11-8">[8]</a></sup> Signs and symptoms may appear less than two days after exposure to the virus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CMAJ2014_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-CMAJ2014-6">[6]</a></sup> These may include <a href="/wiki/Cough" title="Cough">coughing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sore_throat" title="Sore throat">sore throat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhinorrhea" title="Rhinorrhea">runny nose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sneeze" title="Sneeze">sneezing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Headache" title="Headache">headache</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fever" title="Fever">fever</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2015_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2015-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eccles2005_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eccles2005-4">[4]</a></sup> People usually recover in seven to ten days,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2015_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2015-3">[3]</a></sup> but some symptoms may last up to three weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heik2003_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Heik2003-7">[7]</a></sup> Occasionally, those with other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Health_problems" title="Health problems">health problems</a> may develop <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2015_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2015-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Well over 200 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virus_strain" title="Virus strain">virus strains</a> are implicated in causing the common cold, with <a href="/wiki/Rhinovirus" title="Rhinovirus">rhinoviruses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coronavirus" title="Coronavirus">coronaviruses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adenoviridae" title="Adenoviridae">adenoviruses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Enterovirus" title="Enterovirus">enteroviruses</a> being the most common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> They spread through the air during close contact with infected people or indirectly through contact with objects in the environment, followed by transfer to the mouth or nose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2015_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2015-3">[3]</a></sup> Risk factors include going to <a href="/wiki/Child_care" title="Child care">child care</a> facilities, <a href="/wiki/Sleep_deprivation" title="Sleep deprivation">not sleeping well</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Psychological_stress" title="Psychological stress">psychological stress</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CMAJ2014_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-CMAJ2014-6">[6]</a></sup> The symptoms are mostly due to the body's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immune" title="Immune">immune</a> response to the infection rather than to <a href="/wiki/Tissue_(biology)" title="Tissue (biology)">tissue destruction</a> by the viruses themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E112_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-E112-15">[15]</a></sup> The symptoms of <a href="/wiki/Influenza" title="Influenza">influenza</a> are similar to those of a cold, although usually more severe and less likely to include a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Runny_nose" title="Runny nose">runny nose</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CMAJ2014_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-CMAJ2014-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is no <a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">vaccine</a> for the common cold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2015_3-8"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2015-3">[3]</a></sup> The primary methods of prevention are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Correct_hand_washing_technique" title="Correct hand washing technique">hand washing</a>; not touching the eyes, nose or mouth with unwashed hands; and <a href="/wiki/Social_distancing" title="Social distancing">staying away from sick people</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2015_3-9"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2015-3">[3]</a></sup> Some evidence supports the use of <a href="/wiki/Surgical_mask" title="Surgical mask">face masks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E209_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-E209-10">[10]</a></sup> There is also no <a href="/wiki/Cure" title="Cure">cure</a>, but the symptoms can be treated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2015_3-10"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2015-3">[3]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">Zinc</a> may reduce the duration and severity of symptoms if started shortly after the onset of symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2016Zinc_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2016Zinc-11">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nonsteroidal_anti-inflammatory_drug" title="Nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory drug">Nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory drugs</a> (NSAIDs) such as <a href="/wiki/Ibuprofen" title="Ibuprofen">ibuprofen</a> may help with pain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim2015_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kim2015-12">[12]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">Antibiotics</a>, however, should not be used, as all colds are caused by viruses,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> and there is no good evidence that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cough_medicine" title="Cough medicine">cough medicines</a> are effective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CMAJ2014_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-CMAJ2014-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mal2017_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mal2017-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>The common cold is the most frequent infectious disease in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E1_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-E1-19">[19]</a></sup> Under normal circumstances, the average adult gets two to three colds a year, while the average child may get six to eight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CE11_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-CE11-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFP07_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-AFP07-13">[13]</a></sup> Infections occur more commonly <a href="/wiki/Common_cold#Weather" title="Common cold">during the winter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2015_3-11"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2015-3">[3]</a></sup> These infections have existed throughout human history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eccles_p._3_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eccles_p._3-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Signs_and_symptoms">Signs and symptoms</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_lady_suffering_from_the_Common_Cold.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1667" data-file-width="2605" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/A_lady_suffering_from_the_Common_Cold.png/220px-A_lady_suffering_from_the_Common_Cold.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/A_lady_suffering_from_the_Common_Cold.png/330px-A_lady_suffering_from_the_Common_Cold.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/A_lady_suffering_from_the_Common_Cold.png/440px-A_lady_suffering_from_the_Common_Cold.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_lady_suffering_from_the_Common_Cold.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Woman with symptoms of the common cold</div></div></div>
<p>The typical symptoms of a cold include <a href="/wiki/Cough" title="Cough">cough</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Runny_nose" title="Runny nose">runny nose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sneeze" title="Sneeze">sneezing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nasal_congestion" title="Nasal congestion">nasal congestion</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Sore_throat" title="Sore throat">sore throat</a>, sometimes accompanied by <a href="/wiki/Myalgia" title="Myalgia">muscle ache</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatigue_(medical)" title="Fatigue (medical)">fatigue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Headache" title="Headache">headache</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anorexia_(symptom)" title="Anorexia (symptom)">loss of appetite</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E24_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-E24-21">[21]</a></sup> A sore throat is present in about 40% of cases, a cough in about 50%,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CE11_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-CE11-8">[8]</a></sup> and muscle ache likewise in about 50%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eccles2005_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Eccles2005-4">[4]</a></sup> In adults, a <a href="/wiki/Fever" title="Fever">fever</a> is generally not present but it is common in infants and young children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eccles2005_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-Eccles2005-4">[4]</a></sup> The cough is usually mild compared to that accompanying <a href="/wiki/Influenza" title="Influenza">influenza</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eccles2005_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-Eccles2005-4">[4]</a></sup> While a cough and a fever indicate a higher likelihood of influenza in adults, a great deal of similarity exists between these two conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> A number of the viruses that cause the common cold may also result in <a href="/wiki/Asymptomatic" title="Asymptomatic">asymptomatic infections</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>The color of the <a href="/wiki/Mucus" title="Mucus">mucus</a> or nasal secretion may vary from clear to yellow to green and does not indicate the class of agent causing the infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Progression">Progression</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cold_symptoms_cdc.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="998" decoding="async" height="119" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Cold_symptoms_cdc.jpg/220px-Cold_symptoms_cdc.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Cold_symptoms_cdc.jpg/330px-Cold_symptoms_cdc.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Cold_symptoms_cdc.jpg/440px-Cold_symptoms_cdc.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cold_symptoms_cdc.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cold symptoms over time</div></div></div>
<p>A cold usually begins with fatigue, a feeling of being chilled, sneezing, and a headache, followed in a couple of days by a runny nose and cough.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E24_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-E24-21">[21]</a></sup> Symptoms may begin within sixteen hours of exposure<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> and typically peak two to four days after onset.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eccles2005_4-5"><a href="#cite_note-Eccles2005-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> They usually resolve in seven to ten days, but some can last for up to three weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heik2003_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Heik2003-7">[7]</a></sup> The average duration of cough is eighteen days<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid23319500_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid23319500-28">[28]</a></sup> and in some cases people develop a <a href="/wiki/Post-viral_cough" title="Post-viral cough">post-viral cough</a> which can linger after the infection is gone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21198555_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21198555-29">[29]</a></sup> In children, the cough lasts for more than ten days in 35–40% of cases and continues for more than 25 days in 10%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Viruses">Viruses</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coronaviruses_004_lores.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1368" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Coronaviruses_004_lores.jpg/220px-Coronaviruses_004_lores.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Coronaviruses_004_lores.jpg/330px-Coronaviruses_004_lores.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Coronaviruses_004_lores.jpg/440px-Coronaviruses_004_lores.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coronaviruses_004_lores.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Coronavirus" title="Coronavirus">Coronaviruses</a> are a group of viruses known for causing the common cold. They have a halo or crown-like (corona) appearance when viewed under an electron microscope.</div></div></div>
<p>The common cold is an infection of the upper <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_tract" title="Respiratory tract">respiratory tract</a> which can be caused by many different viruses. The most commonly implicated is a <a href="/wiki/Rhinovirus" title="Rhinovirus">rhinovirus</a> (30–80%), a type of <a href="/wiki/Picornavirus" title="Picornavirus">picornavirus</a> with 99 known <a href="/wiki/Serotype" title="Serotype">serotypes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Other commonly implicated viruses include <a href="/wiki/Coronavirus#Infection_in_humans" title="Coronavirus">human coronaviruses</a> (≈ 15%),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CecilGoldman2012_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-CecilGoldman2012-33">[33]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Influenza" title="Influenza">influenza viruses</a> (10–15%),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Medscape_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Medscape-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Adenoviridae" title="Adenoviridae">adenoviruses</a> (5%),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Medscape_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Medscape-34">[34]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_respiratory_syncytial_virus" title="Human respiratory syncytial virus">human respiratory syncytial virus</a> (RSV), <a href="/wiki/Enterovirus" title="Enterovirus">enteroviruses</a> other than rhinoviruses, <a href="/wiki/Human_parainfluenza_viruses" title="Human parainfluenza viruses">human parainfluenza viruses</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Human_metapneumovirus" title="Human metapneumovirus">human metapneumovirus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIAID2006_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIAID2006-37">[37]</a></sup> Frequently more than one virus is present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> In total, more than 200 viral types are associated with colds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eccles2005_4-6"><a href="#cite_note-Eccles2005-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transmission">Transmission</span></h3>
<p>The common cold virus is typically transmitted via airborne droplets (aerosols), direct contact with infected nasal secretions, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fomites" title="Fomites">fomites</a> (contaminated objects).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CE11_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-CE11-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cold197_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cold197-39">[39]</a></sup> Which of these routes is of primary importance has not been determined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E211_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-E211-40">[40]</a></sup> The viruses may survive for prolonged periods in the environment (over 18 hours for rhinoviruses) and can be picked up by people's hands and subsequently carried to their eyes or nose where infection occurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cold197_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cold197-39">[39]</a></sup> Transmission from animals is considered highly unlikely; an outbreak documented at a British scientific base on <a href="/wiki/Adelaide_Island" title="Adelaide Island">Adelaide Island</a> after seventeen weeks of isolation was thought to have been caused by transmission from a contaminated object or an asymptomatic human carrier, rather than from the <a href="/wiki/Siberian_Husky" title="Siberian Husky">husky dogs</a> which were also present at the base.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen1973_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allen1973-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Transmission is common in daycare and at school due to the proximity of many children with little immunity and frequently poor hygiene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Text2007_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Text2007-42">[42]</a></sup> These infections are then brought home to other members of the family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Text2007_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Text2007-42">[42]</a></sup> There is no evidence that recirculated air during commercial flight is a method of transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cold197_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cold197-39">[39]</a></sup> People sitting in close proximity appear to be at greater risk of infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E211_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-E211-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rhinovirus-caused colds are most infectious during the first three days of symptoms; they are much less infectious afterwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-contagiousness_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-contagiousness-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Weather">Weather</span></h3>
<p>A common misconception is that one can "catch a cold" simply through prolonged exposure to cold weather.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Although it is now known that colds are viral infections, the prevalence of many such viruses are indeed seasonal, occurring more frequently during cold weather.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> The reason for the seasonality has not been conclusively determined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nhs_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-nhs-46">[46]</a></sup> Possible explanations may include cold temperature-induced changes in the respiratory system,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EcclesPg_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-EcclesPg-47">[47]</a></sup> decreased immune response,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mourtzoukou_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mourtzoukou-48">[48]</a></sup> and low humidity causing an increase in viral transmission rates, perhaps due to dry air allowing small viral droplets to disperse farther and stay in the air longer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>The apparent seasonality may also be due to social factors, such as people spending more time indoors, near infected people,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EcclesPg_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-EcclesPg-47">[47]</a></sup> and specifically children at school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Text2007_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-Text2007-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nhs_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-nhs-46">[46]</a></sup> Although normal exposure to cold does not increase one's risk of infection, severe exposure leading to significant reduction of body temperature (<a href="/wiki/Hypothermia" title="Hypothermia">hypothermia</a>) may put one at a greater risk for the common cold; although controversial, the majority of evidence suggests that it may increase susceptibility to infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mourtzoukou_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mourtzoukou-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Herd_immunity" title="Herd immunity">Herd immunity</a>, generated from previous exposure to cold viruses, plays an important role in limiting viral spread, as seen with younger populations that have greater rates of respiratory infections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E78_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-E78-50">[50]</a></sup> Poor immune function is a risk factor for disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E78_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-E78-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sleep_deprivation" title="Sleep deprivation">Insufficient sleep</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a> have been associated with a greater risk of developing infection following rhinovirus exposure; this is believed to be due to their effects on immune function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breast_feeding" title="Breast feeding">Breast feeding</a> decreases the risk of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acute_otitis_media" title="Acute otitis media">acute otitis media</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lower_respiratory_tract_infections" title="Lower respiratory tract infections">lower respiratory tract infections</a> among other diseases,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> and it is recommended that breast feeding be continued when an infant has a cold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> In the developed world breast feeding may not be protective against the common cold in and of itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NelsonWilliams2007_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-NelsonWilliams2007-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pathophysiology">Pathophysiology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Illu_conducting_passages.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="328" data-file-width="255" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Illu_conducting_passages.svg/170px-Illu_conducting_passages.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Illu_conducting_passages.svg/255px-Illu_conducting_passages.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Illu_conducting_passages.svg/340px-Illu_conducting_passages.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Illu_conducting_passages.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The common cold is a disease of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_respiratory_tract" title="Upper respiratory tract">upper respiratory tract</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The symptoms of the common cold are believed to be primarily related to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immune" title="Immune">immune</a> response to the virus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E112_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-E112-15">[15]</a></sup> The mechanism of this immune response is virus specific. For example, the rhinovirus is typically acquired by direct contact; it binds to humans via <a href="/wiki/ICAM-1" title="ICAM-1">ICAM-1 receptors</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cadherin_related_family_member_3" title="Cadherin related family member 3">CDHR3 receptor</a> through unknown mechanisms to trigger the release of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inflammatory_mediators" title="Inflammatory mediators">inflammatory mediators</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E112_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-E112-15">[15]</a></sup> These inflammatory mediators then produce the symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E112_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-E112-15">[15]</a></sup> It does not generally cause damage to the nasal <a href="/wiki/Epithelium" title="Epithelium">epithelium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eccles2005_4-7"><a href="#cite_note-Eccles2005-4">[4]</a></sup> The respiratory syncytial virus (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_respiratory_syncytial_virus" title="Human respiratory syncytial virus">RSV</a>), on the other hand, is contracted by direct contact and airborne droplets. It then replicates in the nose and throat before frequently spreading to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lower_respiratory_tract" title="Lower respiratory tract">lower respiratory tract</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E116_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-E116-57">[57]</a></sup> RSV does cause epithelium damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E116_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-E116-57">[57]</a></sup> Human <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parainfluenza" title="Parainfluenza">parainfluenza</a> virus typically results in inflammation of the nose, throat, and <a href="/wiki/Bronchus" title="Bronchus">bronchi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E122_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-E122-58">[58]</a></sup> In young children when it affects the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertebrate_trachea" title="Vertebrate trachea">trachea</a> it may produce the symptoms of <a href="/wiki/Croup" title="Croup">croup</a> due to the small size of their airways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E122_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-E122-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">Diagnosis</span></h2>
<p>The distinction between viral <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_respiratory_tract_infections" title="Upper respiratory tract infections">upper respiratory tract infections</a> is loosely based on the location of symptoms, with the common cold affecting primarily the nose (rhinitis), throat (pharyngitis), and lungs (bronchitis).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CE11_8-5"><a href="#cite_note-CE11-8">[8]</a></sup> There can be significant overlap, and more than one area can be affected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CE11_8-6"><a href="#cite_note-CE11-8">[8]</a></sup> Self-diagnosis is frequent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eccles2005_4-8"><a href="#cite_note-Eccles2005-4">[4]</a></sup> Isolation of the viral agent involved is rarely performed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E51_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-E51-59">[59]</a></sup> and it is generally not possible to identify the virus type through symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eccles2005_4-9"><a href="#cite_note-Eccles2005-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span></h2>
<p>The only useful ways to reduce the spread of cold viruses are physical measures<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E209_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-E209-10">[10]</a></sup> such as using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Correct_hand_washing_technique" title="Correct hand washing technique">correct hand washing technique</a> and face masks; in the healthcare environment, gowns and disposable gloves are also used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E209_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-E209-10">[10]</a></sup> Isolation or <a href="/wiki/Quarantine" title="Quarantine">quarantine</a> is not used as the disease is so widespread and symptoms are non-specific. There is no vaccine to protect against the common cold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">Vaccination</a> has proven difficult as there are many viruses involved and they <a href="/wiki/Viral_evolution" title="Viral evolution">mutate</a> rapidly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E209_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-E209-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Creation of a broadly effective vaccine is, therefore, highly improbable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Regular hand washing appears to be effective in reducing the transmission of cold viruses, especially among children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[63]</a></sup> Whether the addition of <a href="/wiki/Antiviral_drug" title="Antiviral drug">antivirals</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibacterial" title="Antibacterial">antibacterials</a> to normal hand washing provides greater benefit is unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[63]</a></sup> Wearing face masks when around people who are infected may be beneficial; however, there is insufficient evidence for maintaining a greater <a href="/wiki/Social_distancing" title="Social distancing">social distance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is unclear if <a href="/wiki/Zinc#Biological_role" title="Zinc">zinc</a> supplements affect the likelihood of contracting a cold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cochrane2013_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cochrane2013-64">[64]</a></sup> Routine <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">vitamin C</a> supplements do not reduce the risk or severity of the common cold, though they may reduce its duration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hem2013_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hem2013-65">[65]</a></sup> Gargling with water was found useful in one small trial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Management">Management</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pneumonia_strikes_like_a_man_eating_shark.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="678" decoding="async" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Pneumonia_strikes_like_a_man_eating_shark.jpg/170px-Pneumonia_strikes_like_a_man_eating_shark.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Pneumonia_strikes_like_a_man_eating_shark.jpg/255px-Pneumonia_strikes_like_a_man_eating_shark.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Pneumonia_strikes_like_a_man_eating_shark.jpg/340px-Pneumonia_strikes_like_a_man_eating_shark.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pneumonia_strikes_like_a_man_eating_shark.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Poster from 1937 encouraging citizens to "consult your physician" for treatment of the common cold</div></div></div>
<p>Treatments of the common cold primarily involve <a href="/wiki/Symptomatic_treatment" title="Symptomatic treatment">medications and other therapies for symptomatic relief</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFP07_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-AFP07-13">[13]</a></sup> Getting plenty of rest, drinking fluids to maintain hydration, and <a href="/wiki/Gargling" title="Gargling">gargling</a> with warm salt water are reasonable conservative measures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIAID2006_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-NIAID2006-37">[37]</a></sup> Much of the benefit from symptomatic treatment is, however, attributed to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Placebo_effect" title="Placebo effect">placebo effect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> As of 2010,<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Common_cold&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> no medications or herbal remedies had been conclusively demonstrated to shorten the duration of infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Symptomatic">Symptomatic</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Treatments_for_the_Common_Cold_(37652567755).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2444" data-file-width="3220" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Treatments_for_the_Common_Cold_%2837652567755%29.jpg/220px-Treatments_for_the_Common_Cold_%2837652567755%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Treatments_for_the_Common_Cold_%2837652567755%29.jpg/330px-Treatments_for_the_Common_Cold_%2837652567755%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Treatments_for_the_Common_Cold_%2837652567755%29.jpg/440px-Treatments_for_the_Common_Cold_%2837652567755%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Treatments_for_the_Common_Cold_(37652567755).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Various treatments for the common cold - liquid and pill cold medicine, tea, throat lozenges, and over-the-counter decongestants.</div></div></div>
<p>Treatments that may help with symptoms include simple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Analgesics" title="Analgesics">pain medication</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antipyretic" title="Antipyretic">medications for fevers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Ibuprofen" title="Ibuprofen">ibuprofen</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim2015_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kim2015-12">[12]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Paracetamol" title="Paracetamol">acetaminophen (paracetamol)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> It, however, is not clear if acetaminophen helps with symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> It is not known if <a href="/wiki/Over-the-counter_drug" title="Over-the-counter drug">over the counter</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cough_medicine" title="Cough medicine">cough medications</a> are effective for treating an <a href="/wiki/Acute_(medicine)" title="Acute (medicine)">acute</a> cough.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Cough medicines are not recommended for use in children due to a lack of evidence supporting effectiveness and the potential for harm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CFP09_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-CFP09-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> In 2009, Canada restricted the use of <a href="/wiki/Over-the-counter_drug" title="Over-the-counter drug">over-the-counter</a> cough and cold medication in children six years and under due to concerns regarding risks and unproven benefits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CFP09_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-CFP09-72">[72]</a></sup> The misuse of <a href="/wiki/Dextromethorphan" title="Dextromethorphan">dextromethorphan</a> (an over-the-counter cough medicine) has led to its ban in a number of countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Corticosteroid" title="Corticosteroid">Intranasal corticosteroids</a> have not been found to be useful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>In adults short term use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nasal_decongestants" title="Nasal decongestants">nasal decongestants</a> may have a small benefit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deckx2016_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Deckx2016-76">[76]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Antihistamine" title="Antihistamine">Antihistamines</a> may improve symptoms in the first day or two; however, there is no longer-term benefit and they have adverse effects such as drowsiness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Other decongestants such as <a href="/wiki/Pseudoephedrine" title="Pseudoephedrine">pseudoephedrine</a> appear effective in adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deckx2016_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Deckx2016-76">[76]</a></sup> Combined oral analgesics, antihistaminics and decongestants are generally effective for older children and adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ipratropium" title="Ipratropium">Ipratropium</a> nasal spray may reduce the symptoms of a runny nose but has little effect on stuffiness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Ipratropium may also help with cough in adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> The safety and effectiveness of nasal decongestant use in children is unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deckx2016_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-Deckx2016-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to lack of studies, it is not known whether increased fluid intake improves symptoms or shortens respiratory illness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> As of 2017 heated and humidified air, such as via RhinoTherm, is of unclear benefit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> One study has found <a href="/wiki/Chest_rub" title="Chest rub">chest vapor rub</a> to provide some relief of nocturnal cough, congestion, and sleep difficulty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21059712_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21059712-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some advise to avoid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_exercise" title="Physical exercise">physical exercise</a> if there are symptoms such as fever, widespread <a href="/wiki/Myalgia" title="Myalgia">muscle aches</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatigue_(medical)" title="Fatigue (medical)">fatigue</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mayo_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-mayo-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acsm_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-acsm-86">[86]</a></sup> It is regarded as safe to perform moderate exercise if the symptoms are confined to the <a href="/wiki/Head" title="Head">head</a>, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Runny_nose" title="Runny nose">runny nose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nasal_congestion" title="Nasal congestion">nasal congestion</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sneezing" title="Sneezing">sneezing</a>, or a minor <a href="/wiki/Sore_throat" title="Sore throat">sore throat</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mayo_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-mayo-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acsm_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-acsm-86">[86]</a></sup> There is a popular belief that having a hot drink can help with cold symptoms, but evidence to support this is very limited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antibiotics_and_antivirals">Antibiotics and antivirals</span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotics" title="Antibiotics">Antibiotics</a> have no effect against viral infections, including the common cold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CochraneAR2013_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-CochraneAR2013-88">[88]</a></sup> Due to their side effects, antibiotics cause overall harm but are still frequently prescribed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CochraneAR2013_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-CochraneAR2013-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Some of the reasons that antibiotics are so commonly prescribed include people's expectations for them, physicians' desire to help, and the difficulty in excluding complications that may be amenable to antibiotics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> There are no effective <a href="/wiki/Antiviral_drug" title="Antiviral drug">antiviral drugs</a> for the common cold even though some preliminary research has shown benefits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFP07_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-AFP07-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EcclesPg_b_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-EcclesPg_b-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Zinc">Zinc</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Zinc_and_the_common_cold" title="Zinc and the common cold">Zinc and the common cold</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zinc_supplements" title="Zinc supplements">Zinc supplements</a> may shorten the duration of colds by up to 33% and reduce the severity of symptoms if supplementation begins within 24 hours of the onset of symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2016Zinc_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2016Zinc-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cochrane2013_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cochrane2013-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinc_CC_2018_SystRev_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zinc_CC_2018_SystRev-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hemila_2017a_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hemila_2017a-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hemila_2016_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hemila_2016-94">[94]</a></sup> Some zinc remedies directly applied to the inside of the nose have led to the <a href="/wiki/Anosmia" title="Anosmia">loss of the sense of smell</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2016Zinc_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2016Zinc-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> A 2017 review did not recommend the use of zinc for the common cold for various reasons;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mal2017_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mal2017-18">[18]</a></sup> whereas a 2017 and 2018 review both recommended the use of zinc, but also advocated further research on the topic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinc_CC_2018_SystRev_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zinc_CC_2018_SystRev-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hemila_2017a_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hemila_2017a-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_medicine">Alternative medicine</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alternative_treatments_used_for_the_common_cold" title="Alternative treatments used for the common cold">Alternative treatments used for the common cold</a></div>
<p>While there are many <a href="/wiki/Alternative_medicine" title="Alternative medicine">alternative medicines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">Chinese herbal medicines</a> supposed to treat the common cold, there is insufficient <a href="/wiki/Evidence-based_medicine" title="Evidence-based medicine">scientific evidence</a> to support their use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFP07_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-AFP07-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> As of 2015, there is weak evidence to support <a href="/wiki/Nasal_irrigation" title="Nasal irrigation">nasal irrigation</a> with <a href="/wiki/Saline_(medicine)" title="Saline (medicine)">saline</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> There is no firm evidence that <a href="/wiki/Echinacea" title="Echinacea">Echinacea</a> products or <a href="/wiki/Garlic" title="Garlic">garlic</a> provide any meaningful benefit in treating or preventing colds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vitamins_C_and_D">Vitamins C and D</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C_and_the_common_cold" title="Vitamin C and the common cold">Vitamin C and the common cold</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_D_and_respiratory_tract_infections" title="Vitamin D and respiratory tract infections">Vitamin D and respiratory tract infections</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">Vitamin C</a> <a href="/wiki/Dietary_supplement" title="Dietary supplement">supplementation</a> does not affect the incidence of the common cold, but may reduce its duration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hem2013_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hem2013-65">[65]</a></sup> There is no conclusive evidence that <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_D" title="Vitamin D">vitamin D</a> supplementation is efficacious in the prevention or treatment of respiratory tract infections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prognosis">Prognosis</span></h2>
<p>The common cold is generally mild and self-limiting with most symptoms generally improving in a week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CE11_8-7"><a href="#cite_note-CE11-8">[8]</a></sup> In children, half of cases go away in 10 days and 90% in 15 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Severe complications, if they occur, are usually in the very old, the very young, or those who are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immunosuppressed" title="Immunosuppressed">immunosuppressed</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E1_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-E1-19">[19]</a></sup> Secondary bacterial infections may occur resulting in <a href="/wiki/Sinusitis" title="Sinusitis">sinusitis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pharyngitis" title="Pharyngitis">pharyngitis</a>, or an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acute_otitis_media" title="Acute otitis media">ear infection</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> It is estimated that sinusitis occurs in 8% and ear infection in 30% of cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EcclesPg_a_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-EcclesPg_a-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span></h2>
<p>The common cold is the most common human disease<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E1_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-E1-19">[19]</a></sup> and affects people all over the globe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Text2007_42-3"><a href="#cite_note-Text2007-42">[42]</a></sup> Adults typically have two to three infections annually,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CE11_8-8"><a href="#cite_note-CE11-8">[8]</a></sup> and children may have six to ten colds a year (and up to twelve colds a year for school children).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFP07_13-5"><a href="#cite_note-AFP07-13">[13]</a></sup> Rates of symptomatic infections increase in the elderly due to declining immunity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E78_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-E78-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Native Americans and <a href="/wiki/Inuit" title="Inuit">Inuit</a> are more likely to be infected with colds and develop complications such as <a href="/wiki/Otitis_media" title="Otitis media">otitis media</a> than Caucasians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Medscape_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-Medscape-34">[34]</a></sup> This may be explained as much by issues such as poverty and overcrowding as by ethnicity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Medscape_34-3"><a href="#cite_note-Medscape-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Cost_Of_The_Common_Cold_%26_Influenza.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="575" data-file-width="377" decoding="async" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/The_Cost_Of_The_Common_Cold_%26_Influenza.jpg/170px-The_Cost_Of_The_Common_Cold_%26_Influenza.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/The_Cost_Of_The_Common_Cold_%26_Influenza.jpg/255px-The_Cost_Of_The_Common_Cold_%26_Influenza.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/The_Cost_Of_The_Common_Cold_%26_Influenza.jpg/340px-The_Cost_Of_The_Common_Cold_%26_Influenza.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Cost_Of_The_Common_Cold_%26_Influenza.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A British poster from <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> describing the cost of the common cold<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>While the cause of the common cold was identified in the 1950s, the disease appears to have been with humanity since its early history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eccles_p._3_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eccles_p._3-20">[20]</a></sup> Its symptoms and treatment are described in the Egyptian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ebers_papyrus" title="Ebers papyrus">Ebers papyrus</a>, the oldest existing medical text, written before the 16th century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> The name "cold" came into use in the 16th century, due to the similarity between its symptoms and those of exposure to cold weather.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United Kingdom, the <a href="/wiki/Common_Cold_Unit" title="Common Cold Unit">Common Cold Unit</a> (CCU) was set up by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_Research_Council_(UK)" title="Medical Research Council (UK)">Medical Research Council</a> in 1946 and it was where the rhinovirus was discovered in 1956.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> In the 1970s, the CCU demonstrated that treatment with <a href="/wiki/Interferon" title="Interferon">interferon</a> during the incubation phase of rhinovirus infection protects somewhat against the disease,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid2438740_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid2438740-108">[108]</a></sup> but no practical treatment could be developed. The unit was closed in 1989, two years after it completed research of <a href="/wiki/Zinc_gluconate#Zinc_gluconate_and_the_common_cold" title="Zinc gluconate">zinc gluconate lozenges</a> in the prevention and treatment of rhinovirus colds, the only successful treatment in the history of the unit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research_directions">Research directions</span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Antiviral_drug" title="Antiviral drug">Antivirals</a> have been tested for effectiveness in the common cold; as of 2009, none had been both found effective and licensed for use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EcclesPg_b_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-EcclesPg_b-91">[91]</a></sup> There are ongoing trials of the anti-viral drug <a href="/wiki/Pleconaril" title="Pleconaril">pleconaril</a> which shows promise against <a href="/wiki/Picornavirus" title="Picornavirus">picornaviruses</a> as well as trials of BTA-798.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E226_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-E226-110">[110]</a></sup> The oral form of pleconaril had safety issues and an aerosol form is being studied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E226_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-E226-110">[110]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DRACO_(antiviral)" title="DRACO (antiviral)">Double-stranded RNA activated caspase oligomerizer</a> (DRACO), a broad-spectrum antiviral therapy, has shown preliminary effectiveness in treating rhinovirus, as well as other infectious viruses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21818340_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21818340-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genomes</a> of all known human rhinovirus strains have been sequenced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CTgov_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-CTgov-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Societal_impact">Societal impact</span></h2>
<p>The economic impact of the common cold is not well understood in much of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EcclesPg_a_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-EcclesPg_a-103">[103]</a></sup> In the United States, the common cold leads to 75–100 million physician visits annually at a conservative cost estimate of $7.7 billion per year. Americans spend $2.9 billion on over-the-counter drugs and another $400 million on prescription medicines for symptom relief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frend03_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frend03-113">[113]</a></sup> More than one-third of people who saw a doctor received an antibiotic prescription, which has implications for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotic_resistance" title="Antibiotic resistance">antibiotic resistance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frend03_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-Frend03-113">[113]</a></sup> An estimated 22–189 million school days are missed annually due to a cold. As a result, parents missed 126 million workdays to stay home to care for their children. When added to the 150 million workdays missed by employees who have a cold, the total economic impact of cold-related work loss exceeds $20 billion per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIAID2006_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-NIAID2006-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frend03_113-2"><a href="#cite_note-Frend03-113">[113]</a></sup> This accounts for 40% of time lost from work in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>

href="#cite_ref-CE11_8-8"><sup><i><b>i</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFArroll2011">Arroll B (March 2011). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3275147" rel="nofollow">"Common cold"</a>. <i>BMJ Clinical Evidence</i>. <b>2011</b> (3): 1510. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3275147" rel="nofollow">3275147</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/21406124" rel="nofollow">21406124</a>. <q>Common colds are defined as upper respiratory tract infections that affect the predominantly nasal part of the respiratory mucosa</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=BMJ+Clinical+Evidence&amp;rft.atitle=Common+cold&amp;rft.volume=2011&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.pages=1510&amp;rft.date=2011-03&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3275147%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F21406124&amp;rft.aulast=Arroll&amp;rft.aufirst=B&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3275147&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACommon+cold"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://www.mayoclinic.org/diseases-conditions/bronchiolitis/symptoms-causes/syc-20351565" rel="nofollow">"Bronchiolitis: Symptoms and Causes"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Mayo_Clinic" title="Mayo Clinic">Mayo Clinic</a></i><span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-E209-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-E209_10-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-E209_10-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-E209_10-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-E209_10-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-E209_10-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Eccles p. 209</span>

<li id="cite_note-NIH2016Zinc-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-NIH2016Zinc_11-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-NIH2016Zinc_11-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-NIH2016Zinc_11-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-NIH2016Zinc_11-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://ods.od.nih.gov/factsheets/Zinc-HealthProfessional" rel="nofollow">"Zinc – Fact Sheet for Health Professionals"</a>. Office of Dietary Supplements, US National Institutes of Health. 10 July 2019<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">27 December</span> 2019</span>. <q>Although studies examining the effect of zinc treatment on cold symptoms have had somewhat conflicting results, overall zinc appears to be beneficial under certain circumstances.... In September of 2007, Caruso and colleagues published a structured review of the effects of zinc lozenges, nasal sprays, and nasal gels on the common cold [69]. Of the 14 randomized, placebo-controlled studies included, 7 (5 using zinc lozenges, 2 using a nasal gel) showed that the zinc treatment had a beneficial effect and 7 (5 using zinc lozenges, 1 using a nasal spray, and 1 using lozenges and a nasal spray) showed no effect. More recently, a Cochrane review concluded that "zinc (lozenges or syrup) is beneficial in reducing the duration and severity of the common cold in healthy people, when taken within 24 hours of onset of symptoms" [73]. The author of another review completed in 2004 also concluded that zinc can reduce the duration and severity of cold symptoms [68]. However, more research is needed to determine the optimal dosage, zinc formulation and duration of treatment before a general recommendation for zinc in the treatment of the common cold can be made [73]. As previously noted, the safety of intranasal zinc has been called into question because of numerous reports of anosmia (loss of smell), in some cases long-lasting or permanent, from the use of zinc-containing nasal gels or sprays [17–19].</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Zinc+%E2%80%93+Fact+Sheet+for+Health+Professionals&amp;rft.pub=Office+of+Dietary+Supplements%2C+US+National+Institutes+of+Health&amp;rft.date=2019-07-10&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fods.od.nih.gov%2Ffactsheets%2FZinc-HealthProfessional&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACommon+cold"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Kim2015-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Kim2015_12-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Kim2015_12-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Kim2015_12-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFKimChangChoHwang2015">Kim SY, Chang YJ, Cho HM, Hwang YW, Moon YS (September 2015). "Non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs for the common cold". <i>The Cochrane Database of Systematic Reviews</i>. <b>9</b> (9): CD006362. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1002%2F14651858.CD006362.pub4" rel="nofollow">10.1002/14651858.CD006362.pub4</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26387658" rel="nofollow">26387658</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Cochrane+Database+of+Systematic+Reviews&amp;rft.atitle=Non-steroidal+anti-inflammatory+drugs+for+the+common+cold&amp;rft.volume=9&amp;rft.issue=9&amp;rft.pages=CD006362&amp;rft.date=2015-09&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1002%2F14651858.CD006362.pub4&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F26387658&amp;rft.aulast=Kim&amp;rft.aufirst=SY&amp;rft.au=Chang%2C+YJ&amp;rft.au=Cho%2C+HM&amp;rft.au=Hwang%2C+YW&amp;rft.au=Moon%2C+YS&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACommon+cold"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Mal2017-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Mal2017_18-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Mal2017_18-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFMaleskerCallahan-LyonIrelandIrwin2017">Malesker MA, Callahan-Lyon P, Ireland B, Irwin RS (November 2017). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC6026258" rel="nofollow">"Pharmacologic and Nonpharmacologic Treatment for Acute Cough Associated With the Common Cold: CHEST Expert Panel Report"</a>. <i>Chest</i>. <b>152</b> (5): 1021–1037. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.chest.2017.08.009" rel="nofollow">10.1016/j.chest.2017.08.009</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC6026258" rel="nofollow">6026258</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/28837801" rel="nofollow">28837801</a>. <q>A suggestion for the use of zinc lozenges in healthy adults with cough due to common cold was considered by the expert panel. However, due to weak evidence, the potential side effects of zinc, and the relatively benign and common nature of the condition being treated, the panel did not approve inclusion of this suggestion.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Chest&amp;rft.atitle=Pharmacologic+and+Nonpharmacologic+Treatment+for+Acute+Cough+Associated+With+the+Common+Cold%3A+CHEST+Expert+Panel+Report&amp;rft.volume=152&amp;rft.issue=5&amp;rft.pages=1021-1037&amp;rft.date=2017-11&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC6026258%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F28837801&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2Fj.chest.2017.08.009&amp;rft.aulast=Malesker&amp;rft.aufirst=MA&amp;rft.au=Callahan-Lyon%2C+P&amp;rft.au=Ireland%2C+B&amp;rft.au=Irwin%2C+RS&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC6026258&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACommon+cold"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-35">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://www.hmsreview.org/issue/2015/1/the-most-common-illness-a-review-and-case-study-from-harvard-medical-school" rel="nofollow">"The Most Common Illness: A Review and Case Study from Harvard Medical School"</a>. <i>HMSR</i>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20170405021854/http://hmsreview.org/issue/2015/1/the-most-common-illness-a-review-and-case-study-from-harvard-medical-school" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 5 April 2017<span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-36">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFHeikkinenJärvinen2003">Heikkinen T, Järvinen A (January 2003). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC7112468" rel="nofollow">"The common cold"</a>. <i>Lancet</i>. <b>361</b> (9351): 51–9. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2FS0140-6736%2803%2912162-9" rel="nofollow">10.1016/S0140-6736(03)12162-9</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC7112468" rel="nofollow">7112468</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/12517470" rel="nofollow">12517470</a>. <q>Influenza is often regarded as a disease entity separate from the common cold.
